Ghusl for Intercourse with a Small Girl: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ghusl condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition for ghusl when a small girl is penetrated and distinguish the three intra-Hanafi positions (always wajib, never wajib, wajib only if penetration possible and hymen not broken).

Core Idea
Ghusl for intercourse with a small girl depends on actual penetration, not mere contact.

In this lesson, we examine a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute about the obligation of ghusl (ritual bath) after intercourse with a young girl who does not yet experience desire. The source, from al-Bahr al-Raiq, presents three positions and settles on a nuanced view that links ghusl to the possibility of penetration and the integrity of the hymen. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to discern when physical contact triggers full purification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
Recognize that the act is intercourse (wat') with a small girl (saghira) who does not feel desire. The dispute is about whether ghusl is required. Note that the general rule for intercourse is that ghusl is wajib upon insertion of the glans (al-taqa'ud). Here the issue is whether this rule applies to a prepubescent girl.
The girl's lack of desire is relevant only to the dispute's framing, not to the ruling per se.
2
List the three intra-Hanafi positions
The text reports: (1) Some say ghusl is always wajib. (2) Some say it is never wajib. (3) The sahih (correct) view: ghusl is wajib if penetration is possible (amkana al-ilaj) in the place of intercourse and the hymen is not broken (lam yafvidha). If the hymen remains intact, that indicates no actual penetration, so ghusl is not wajib.
The third view is itself compound: it requires both possibility and non-defloration.
3
Examine the evidence for the sahih view
The text attributes the sahih view to al-Sayrafi in al-Idah. The evidence is talil (reasoning): if penetration is possible and the hymen is unbroken, she is considered 'one who can be copulated with' (mimmā tujāma'), so the general rule of ghusl applies. Conversely, if the hymen is intact, it serves as proof (dalil) that no actual penetration occurred, so the general rule does not apply. This latter point is cited from al-Nihaya attributing to al-Muhit.
The hymen (bakara) functions as circumstantial evidence (qarina) for the fact of penetration.
4
Understand the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The difference matters when actual penetration is uncertain. If the hymen remains intact after a man claims he inserted only the glans but did not penetrate fully, the latter view would rule no ghusl, while the first view (always wajib) would require it. The sahih view balances the two: if penetration was possible and hymen remains, ghusl is wajib because possibility is enough; but if the hymen is intact as a sign of no penetration, ghusl is not required. This appears contradictory until you realize the sahih view considers 'possibility' as the trigger, and the hymen evidence is used only when possibility is absent.
The phrase 'lam yafvidha' (did not deflower her) is ambiguous: it could mean he did not break the hymen, or that he did not fully penetrate. The text uses it to mean he did not break the hymen, thus no actual penetration.
5
Identify the relied-upon position (mufta bihi)
The text explicitly states 'as-sahih' (the correct/correct view) as the position with conditions. The other view from al-Nihaya (no ghusl if hymen remains) is noted but not adopted. Thus, for fatwa, the operative ruling is: ghusl is wajib if penetration was possible in the vaginal area, regardless of whether the hymen was broken, provided that the hymen intactness does not negate the possibility. In practice, if the hymen is still intact after the act, it raises doubt, but the sahih view still obligates ghusl because the condition of 'possibility' is met.
This is a precise point: 'the possibility of insertion' is the criterion, not the actual insertion. If the man inserted, the hymen would likely break, so the case where hymen remains implies the insertion did not occur, but the sahih view focuses on possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد حكى عن السراج الوهاج خلافاً في وطء الصغيرة التي لا تشتهي فمنهم من ومنهم من قال لا يجب مطلقاً والصحيح أنه إذا أمكن الإيلاج في محل الجماع من الصغيرة ولم يفضها فهي ممن تجامع فيجب الغسل وعزاه للصيرفي في الإيضاح وقد يقال : إن بقاء البكارة دليل على عدم الإيلاج فلا يجب الغسل كما اختاره في النهاية معزياً إلى المحيط.
And it was narrated from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj a difference regarding intercourse with a small girl who is not desired; some said it is obligatory absolutely, and some said it is not obligatory at all, and the correct view is that if penetration is possible in the place of intercourse of the small girl and he did not deflower her, then she is one who can be copulated with, so ghusl is obligatory, and he attributed it to al-Sayrafi in al-Idah. And it may be said: The remaining of the hymen is evidence of no penetration, so ghusl is not obligatory, as was chosen in al-Nihaya attributing to al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether ghusl is required for intercourse with a small girl (who does not yet feel desire). Some say it is always obligatory, others say never. The relied-upon (sahih) opinion is that if penetration is possible in the vaginal area (mahall al-jima') and the hymen is not broken (lam yafvidha), then ghusl is required because she is considered capable of being copulated with. However, if the hymen remains intact, that indicates no actual penetration, so ghusl is not obligatory. The latter view from al-Nihaya is also noted. The practical consequence is that ghusl depends on actual penetration, not just insertion of the glans into the vulva.

Key Terms
بكارة (bakara): hymen, a membrane that partially covers the vaginal opening; its intactness is used as evidence regarding penetration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct view (as-sahih) in the passage, when is ghusl obligatory for intercourse with a small girl?
A) Whenever the glans enters the vulva, even if the hymen remains intact.
B) Only if the hymen is broken.
C) If penetration is possible and he did not deflower her.
D) Always, regardless of age.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi on ghusl with a small girl is that ghusl is wajib if penetration is possible, even if the hymen remains intact.

When Does a Cloth Barrier Make Ghusl Wajib? The Hanafi Conditional Ruling
Ruling on ghusl when penis is wrapped with a cloth and inserted

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which ghusl becomes obligatory when a cloth is wrapped around the penis, and apply the Hanafi principle that sensation (feeling heat and pleasure) triggers the obligation.

Core Idea
Ghusl becomes wajib if the cloth is thin enough to feel heat and pleasure — sensation, not mere insertion, triggers the obligation.

In this lesson, we examine a specific case within the chapter of taharah: a man wraps a cloth around his penis before inserting, without ejaculation. The obligation of ghusl depends on the thinness of the cloth and whether sensation is transmitted. The Hanafi school offers a nuanced view that balances the definition of penetration with practical experience.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
The act is inserting the penis wrapped in a cloth without ejaculation. Scholars disagree on whether ghusl is wajib.
2
Understand the two opinions
Some say ghusl is wajib because the action is still called penetration; others say it is not wajib because the barrier prevents direct contact.
3
Apply the asahh (most correct) condition
The preferred view distinguishes based on the cloth's thinness and resulting sensation: if he feels heat and pleasure, ghusl is wajib; otherwise not.
4
Note the precautionary (ahwat) recommendation
The author advises performing ghusl in either case as a precaution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو لف على ذكره خرقة وأولج ولم ينزل قال بعضهم يجب الغسل لأنه يسمى مولجاً، وقال بعضهم لا يجب. والأصح إن كانت الخرقة رقيقة بحيث يجد حرارة الفرج واللذة وجب الغسل وإلا فلا، والأحوط وجوب الغسل في الوجهين
And if he wrapped a cloth on his penis and inserted and did not ejaculate, some said ghusl is obligatory because he is called a penetrator, and some said it is not obligatory. The most correct is that if the cloth is thin such that he feels the heat of the private part and pleasure, ghusl is obligatory; otherwise not. And the most precautionary is the obligation of ghusl in both cases.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage deals with a case where a man wraps a cloth around his penis before inserting into the vagina or anus. There is a difference of opinion: some say ghusl is wajib because the act is still called 'ilaj (penetration), while others say it is not wajib because there is a barrier. The preferred (asahh) view distinguishes based on the thinness of the cloth: if the cloth is thin enough that the man feels the heat and pleasure, then ghusl is wajib; otherwise not. Nevertheless, the cautious position (ahwat) is to perform ghusl in both cases to avoid missing an obligation. This reflects the Hanafi principle that the essence of intercourse (direct contact) triggers ghusl, and when there is doubt, precaution is recommended.

Key Terms
خرقة (kharqa): a cloth used as a barrier during intercourse

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the most correct view, when is ghusl obligatory with a cloth wrapping?
A) Always
B) Only if the cloth is thin and he feels heat and pleasure
C) Only if he ejaculates
D) Never

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi asahh view makes ghusl wajib when a thin cloth transmits sensation; otherwise it is not wajib, though precautionary ghusl is recommended.

Ghusl for Khuntha (Hermaphrodite) Insertion Scenarios
Rulings on ghusl for various insertion scenarios involving a khuntha mushkil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ghusl for insertion involving a khuntha mushkil and explain why ambiguity affects the obligation.

Core Idea
For a khuntha mushkil, insertion alone does not require ghusl because the organ may be extra; only ejaculation triggers obligation.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq addresses a subtle fiqh issue: does insertion of a hermaphrodite's organ into another person require ghusl? The answer hinges on the ambiguity of the khuntha's gender. Because the genitalia may be non-functional or extra, the act may not constitute valid intercourse. Understanding this helps you apply the principle that rulings depend on certainty and that ambiguity can nullify obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
Recognize that the ruling applies specifically to a khuntha mushkil (a hermaphrodite with ambiguous genitalia).
The ambiguity is key; if gender were determined, the normal rules would apply.
2
Understand the legal cause for ghusl
Normally, ghusl becomes wajib due to insertion of the glans into a private part (qabl or dabar) or due to ejaculation. Identify which cause is in doubt.
Insertion alone is the first cause, but it is uncertain here.
3
Analyze the effect of ambiguity
Because the organ may be extra (za'id), the act may not be valid intercourse. Ghusl is not required unless certainty is achieved through ejaculation.
The principle is that certainty is not removed by doubt (al-yaqin la yazulu bi al-shakk).
4
Apply to each scenario
Go through the four scenarios: khuntha into woman, man into khuntha, khuntha into khuntha, man into khuntha's vagina. All yield no ghusl without ejaculation.
In each case, the possibility of the organ being non-genuine is considered.
5
Note the exception
If ejaculation occurs, ghusl becomes wajib because semen discharge is certain, regardless of the organ's status.
Ejaculation confirms sexual activity and necessitates ritual purification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن أولج الخنثى المشكل ذكره في فرج امرأة أو دبرها فلا غسل عليهما الجواز أن يكون امرأة وهذا الذكر منه زائد فيصير كمن أولج أصبعه، وكذا في دبر رجل أو فرج خنثى لجواز أن يكونا رجلين والفرجان زائدان منهما، وكذا في فرج خنثى مثله لجواز أن يكون الخنثى المولج فيه رجلاً والفرج زائد منه . وإن أولج رجل في فرج خنثى مشكل لم يجب الغسل عليه الجواز أن يكون الخنثى رجلاً والفرج منه بمنزلة الجرح. وهذا كله إذا كان من غير إنزال، أما إذا أنزل وجب الغسل بالإنزال كذا في السراج الوهاج. وهذا لا يرد على المصنف لأن كلامه في حشفة وقبل محققين والله أعلم بالصواب.
And if a hermaphrodite with an ambiguous problem inserts his penis into a woman's vagina or anus, then no ghusl is required for either because it is possible that the hermaphrodite is a woman and this penis is extra, so it becomes like one who inserts his finger. Similarly, [if a man inserts into] an anus of a man or the vagina of a hermaphrodite, because it is possible that both are men and the vaginas are extra from them. Similarly, [if a hermaphrodite inserts] into the vagina of a similar hermaphrodite, because it is possible that the hermaphrodite being inserted into is a man and the vagina is extra from him. And if a man inserts into the vagina of an ambiguous hermaphrodite, ghusl is not required for him because it is possible that the hermaphrodite is a man and the vagina is like a wound. All this is if there is no ejaculation; but if he ejaculates, ghusl is obligatory due to ejaculation. This is stated in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. And this does not refute the author because his statement is about the glans and the vagina of verified [women], and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage provides rulings on ghusl in cases involving a khuntha mushkil (a hermaphrodite whose gender cannot be determined). The key principle is that due to the ambiguity, the organ involved may be considered extra (za'id) or non-genuine, thus no ghusl is required for insertion alone, because the act may not be valid intercourse. Four scenarios are outlined: (1) if a khuntha inserts his penis into a woman's vagina or anus, no ghusl because the penis could be extra, (2) if a man inserts into a man's anus or a khuntha's vagina (similar), (3) if a khuntha inserts into another khuntha's vagina, (4) if a man inserts into a khuntha's vagina. In all cases, if ejaculation occurs, ghusl becomes obligatory due to the discharge of semen. The commentator clarifies that this does not contradict the author's main rule because the author was referring to verified male and female organs.

Key Terms
خنثى مشكل (khuntha mushkil): a hermaphrodite with ambiguous genitalia making gender determination impossible

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does ghusl become obligatory in khuntha-related insertions according to this text?
A) Whenever any insertion occurs
B) Only when the active partner is male
C) Only if ejaculation occurs
D) Never

Key Takeaway
For a khuntha mushkil, insertion alone does not obligate ghusl; only ejaculation does, due to the ambiguity of the organs.

The Obligating Cause of Ghusl for Hayd: Blood Flow or Its Cessation?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the cause of obligatory ghusl for hayd and nifas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on the cause of obligatory ghusl for hayd and nifas, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of each view.

Core Idea
Is ghusl due at the sight of menstrual blood or only after it stops? Two Hanafi authorities differ on this key ruling.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses a subtle but important difference within the Hanafi school: what exactly makes ghusl obligatory after menstruation or postpartum bleeding? The author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq appears to have held two different views in two of his works. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to trace how rulings derive from their causes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting positions
The passage presents two views from the same author: (a) from al-Mustasfa, (b) from al-Kafi. Note that both are within the Hanafi school.
This is not a cross-madhhab dispute but an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
2
Analyze the reasoning for the al-Mustasfa view
According to al-Mustasfa, the obligator (mu'jib) is the seeing or exit of blood. The reasoning: blood breaks major purity (janabah-like state), but ghusl is incompatible with flowing blood; once blood stops, ghusl becomes possible and is required due to the prior event.
3
Understand the alternative from al-Kafi
In al-Kafi, the obligator is the cessation of blood, not its exit. The text cuts off, but the implication is that the cessation itself necessitates ghusl.
The full reasoning is missing; you may infer that cessation is a pure state that can be the cause.
4
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the cause is the flow, ghusl becomes due at the first appearance of blood (though it cannot be performed until after). If the cause is cessation, ghusl becomes due only after the blood has completely stopped.
This affects when a woman is considered ritually pure and can resume prayers.
5
Note the status of the dispute
No explicit tarjih (preference) is given in this passage. Later Hanafi works may prefer one view. This shows that even within the school, there can be nuanced differences.
Students should not assume a single Hanafi position on every detail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وحيض ونفاس أي وفرض الغسل عند حيض ونفاس. وقد اختلف رأي المصنف في كتبه هل الموجب الحيض أو انقطاعه؟ فاختار في المستصفى أن الموجب رؤية الدم أو خروجه وعلل بأن الدم إذا حصل نقض الطهارة الكبرى ولم يجب الغسل مع سيلان الدم لأنه ينافيه، فإذا انقطع أمكن الغسل فوجب لأجل ذلك الحدث السابق، فأما الانقطاع فهو طهارة فلا يوجب الطهارة واختار في الكافي أن الموجب انقطاع الدم لا خروجه لأن
His statement: 'and hayd and nifas' means that ghusl is prescribed at hayd and nifas. And the author's opinion differed in his books: whether the obligator is the menses itself or its cessation? He chose in al-Mustasfa that the obligator is the seeing or exit of blood, and he reasoned: when blood occurs, it breaks the major purity, but ghusl is not obligatory while blood is flowing because it is incompatible; so when it stops, ghusl becomes possible and becomes obligatory due to that previous event; as for the cessation, it is purity, so it does not obligate purity. And in al-Kafi he chose that the obligator is the cessation of blood, not its exit, because...
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage addresses a key difference within the Hanafi school regarding the cause of obligatory ghusl for hayd (menses) and nifas (postpartum bleeding). The author (likely the author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq) had two views: (1) In al-Mustasfa: the cause is the appearance of blood, because blood breaks the major ritual purity (janabah-like state), but ghusl cannot be performed while blood is flowing due to contradiction; once blood stops, ghusl becomes possible and is required due to the prior occurrence of blood. (2) In al-Kafi: the cause is the cessation of blood, not the exit. The text cuts off, but the implication is that the cessation itself necessitates ghusl. This difference affects when ghusl becomes due: at the first sight of blood or only after it stops. The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school may vary.

Key Terms
حيض (hayd): menstrual blood
نفاس (nifas): postpartum bleeding
انقطاع (inqita'): cessation of blood

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the view from al-Mustasfa, what is the cause of obligatory ghusl for hayd?
A) The cessation of blood
B) The sight of blood
C) The end of the menstrual period
D) The duration of bleeding

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school contains nuanced internal differences on the cause of obligatory ghusl, reminding us that rulings are derived from careful analysis of causes and consequences.

When Does Ghusl Become Wajib? Insertion Without Ejaculation
Ruling with intra-madhhab dispute and exceptional cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ghusl for insertion without ejaculation and apply the conditions (thin cloth / feeling pleasure) to determine obligation.

Core Idea
Mere insertion without ejaculation does not require ghusl unless pleasure is felt through a thin barrier.

This lesson unpacks a detailed Hanafi ruling from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq about when ritual bath (ghusl) is obligatory after sexual contact. While many assume any penetration triggers ghusl, the Hanafi school takes a precise approach: only ejaculation or proven pleasure through a thin barrier makes ghusl wajib. We will explore the cloth barrier debate and the unusual case of a problematic hermaphrodite, sharpening your ability to apply conditional rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the base principle
Identify that mere insertion (ilaj) without ejaculation does not obligate ghusl, as per al-Nihaya and al-Muhit.
This is the default Hanafi position, consistent with Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Analyze the cloth barrier case
When a cloth is used, there is ikhtilaf: some say ghusl is always obligatory because insertion occurred; others say it depends on sensation. The correct view (al-ashah) ties obligation to the cloth being thin enough to feel heat and pleasure.
The precautionary view (al-ahwat) recommends ghusl in both cases.
3
Examine the hermaphrodite cases
For a problematic hermaphrodite (khuntha mushkil), no ghusl is required due to the possibility that the genitals are superfluous, making the act like inserting a finger.
The ruling hinges on uncertainty of gender, not on the act itself.
4
Note the exception for ejaculation
If ejaculation (inzal) occurs, ghusl becomes wajib regardless of the method, as ejaculation is a direct cause.
This overrides all the above conditional cases.
5
Apply the ruling to a real scenario
Given a case, check: Did ejaculation occur? If yes → ghusl wajib. If no → Did insertion involve a thin cloth with pleasure? If yes → ghusl wajib; if not → ghusl not wajib (but recommended).
This is the mufta bihi framework for fatwa today.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة
الإيلاج فلا يجب الغسل كما اختاره في النهاية معزياً إلى المحيط. ولو لف على ذكره خرقة وأولج ولم ينزل قال بعضهم يجب الغسل لأنه يسمى مولجاً، وقال بعضهم لا يجب. والأصح إن كانت الخرقة رقيقة بحيث يجد حرارة الفرج واللذة وجب الغسل وإلا فلا، والأحوط وجوب الغسل في الوجهين وإن أولج الخنثى المشكل ذكره في فرج امرأة أو دبرها فلا غسل عليهما الجواز أن يكون امرأة وهذا الذكر منه زائد فيصير كمن أولج أصبعه، وكذا في دبر رجل أو فرج خنثى لجواز أن يكونا رجلين والفرجان زائدان منهما، وكذا في فرج خنثى مثله لجواز أن يكون الخنثى المولج فيه رجلاً والفرج زائد منه . وإن أولج رجل في فرج خنثى مشكل لم يجب الغسل عليه الجواز أن يكون الخنثى رجلاً والفرج منه بمنزلة الجرح. وهذا كله إذا كان من غير إنزال، أما إذا أنزل وجب الغسل بالإنزال كذا في السراج الوهاج. وهذا لا يرد على المصنف لأن كلامه في حشفة وقبل محققين والله أعلم بالصواب.
Book of Purity. For insertion, ghusl is not obligatory, as chosen in al-Nihaya attributed to al-Muhit. If one wraps a cloth around his penis and inserts and does not ejaculate, some said ghusl is obligatory because he is called a 'mūlij' (one who inserts), and some said it is not obligatory. The most correct is if the cloth is thin such that he feels the heat of the vagina and pleasure, then ghusl is obligatory; otherwise not, and the most precautionary is the obligation of ghusl in both cases. If a problematic hermaphrodite inserts his penis into a woman's vagina or anus, there is no ghusl on either because of the possibility that (the hermaphrodite) is a woman and this penis is a superfluous growth, thus he becomes like one who inserts his finger. Similarly, into a man's anus or a hermaphrodite's vagina, because of the possibility that both are men and the two genitals are superfluous. Similarly, into the vagina of an hermaphrodite like him, because of the possibility that the hermaphrodite being inserted into is a man and the vagina is superfluous. If a man inserts into the vagina of a problematic hermaphrodite, ghusl is not obligatory on him because of the possibility that the hermaphrodite is a man and the vagina is like a wound. All this is if there is no ejaculation; if he ejaculates, ghusl becomes obligatory due to ejaculation. This is as stated in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. This does not refute the author, because his statement is about a definite glans and vulva. And Allah knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discusses the rules of ghusl (ritual bath) for insertion (ilaj) without ejaculation. The passage is a ruling with proof and explanation. The author explains that mere insertion does not necessitate ghusl, as chosen by the author of al-Nihaya citing al-Muhit. He then examines the case where a cloth is wrapped around the penis before insertion. There is disagreement: some say ghusl is required because the act is still called 'insertion'; others say no. The correct position (al-ashah) depends on the thinness of the cloth: if it is thin enough that the man feels heat and pleasure, ghusl is required; otherwise not. Nonetheless, it is precautionary (ahwat) to perform ghusl in both cases. Next, the passage addresses the problematic hermaphrodite (khuntha mushkil). Since the true gender is unknown, the ruling assumes the possibility that the khuntha might be female, in which case his penis is considered a superfluous growth (zā'id), making the act like inserting a finger—thus no ghusl. Similarly, if the partner is also ambiguous, the same principle applies. The key condition is that no ghusl is needed if there is no ejaculation; if ejaculation occurs, ghusl is obligatory due to the emission of semen. The passage concludes that this discussion does not conflict with the author's (Ibn Nujaym's) original matn, which speaks about a definite glans and vulva (i.e., clear male and female).

Key Terms
الإيلاج (al-ilāj): Insertion of the glans (or equivalent) into the vagina or anus.
الخرقة (al-khirqa): A cloth or barrier between the penis and the partner's body during intercourse.
الخنثى المشكل (al-khunthā al-mushkil): A person whose gender cannot be definitively determined due to ambiguous genitalia.
الإنزال (al-inzāl): Ejaculation of semen.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does ghusl become wajib if a man wraps a cloth around his penis and inserts without ejaculating?
A) Whenever the cloth is thin.
B) Only if he feels heat and pleasure through the thin cloth.
C) Never, because there was no ejaculation.
D) Only if the cloth is thick.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, ghusl is not obligatory for mere insertion without ejaculation unless a thin barrier allows sexual pleasure to be felt.

The Cause of Ghusl from Menstruation: Blood or Cessation?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the cause of ghusl from hayd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three Hanafi positions on the cause of ghusl from menstruation and identify the practical consequences (thamarah al-khilaf) of each view.

Core Idea
The obligation of ghusl from menstruation is caused by the blood, not its cessation; cessation is a condition for performance.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute: does the very blood of menstruation obligate ghusl, or does its cessation? The answer influences rulings like whether a female martyr is washed and whether a woman sins for delaying ghusl. We will trace the arguments of Ibn Nujaym (who held both views in different works), the critiques of later scholars, and the preferred position according to al-Hidayah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing positions
First, note that Ibn Nujaym in al-Mustasfa held that the cause is the exit of blood; in al-Kafi he held that it is cessation. Understand the reasoning for each.
The first view: blood nullifies purity but ghusl is postponed until cessation. The second: cessation itself triggers the duty.
2
Examine the third synthetic position
The author (Ibn Nujaym in this commentary) suggests that ghusl is not obligatory until an act requiring purity (e.g., prayer) becomes due. This is supported by consensus that there is no sin before prayer time.
This view renders the dispute less significant for sin but still relevant for other consequences.
3
Analyze the practical consequences (thamarah al-khilaf)
The passage lists two concrete benefits: (a) conditional divorce (ta'liq) and (b) washing a female martyr who dies before cessation. Understand how each position yields a different ruling in these cases.
For the martyr: blood-cause view says she is washed; cessation-cause view says she is not.
4
Determine the tarjih (preferred view)
Al-Hidayah is cited as correcting the martyr case — she is washed, which supports the blood-as-cause view. This indicates the relied-upon position.
The mufta bihi thus aligns with the exit of blood as the cause, with cessation as a condition.
5
Understand the subsidiary dispute on sin
The passage mentions a secondary ikhtilaf between Iraqi (al-Karkhi) and Bukhari scholars on whether a woman sins if she delays ghusl after cessation. This is linked to the broader cause dispute.
Iraqis tie sin to delay after cessation; Bukharis tie it to delay of prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وحيض ونفاس أي وفرض الغسل عند حيض ونفاس. وقد اختلف رأي المصنف في كتبه هل الموجب الحيض أو انقطاعه؟ فاختار في المستصفى أن الموجب رؤية الدم أو خروجه وعلل بأن الدم إذا حصل نقض الطهارة الكبرى ولم يجب الغسل مع سيلان الدم لأنه ينافيه، فإذا انقطع أمكن الغسل فوجب لأجل ذلك الحدث السابق، فأما الانقطاع فهو طهارة فلا يوجب الطهارة واختار في الكافي أن الموجب انقطاع الدم لا خروجه لأن كذلك. ولذا قالوا: لو جومعت البكر لا غسل عليها، إلا إذا حملت لإنزالها، إنما الكلام في أن الغسل هل يجب بوطء الصغيرة حيث لا مانع إلا الصغر اختلفوا، والصحيح أنها لو كانت بحيث تفضي بالوطء لم يجب وإن توارت الحشفة لقصور الداعي وإلا وجب اهـ وحاصله تقييد قول السراج فيجب الغسل إذا لم يفضها بشرط زوال عذرتها لا مطلقاً وهو كلام حسن سوى قوله «إلا إذا حملت» لما علمت مما تقدم ما فيه قوله : ( وإن أولج الخنثى المشكل ذكره في فرج امرأة الخ) قال الشرنبلالي في شرح نور الإيضاح الكبير قلت ويشكل عليه معاملة الخنثى بالأضر في أحواله وعليه يلزمه الغسل اهـ. أقول: معاملته بالأضر والأحوط ليس على سبيل الوجوب دائماً بل قد يكون مستحباً في مواضع منها هذه، ووجهه أن إشكاله أورث شبهة وهي لا ترفع الثابت بيقين لأن الطهارة كانت ثابتة يقيناً فلا ترتفع بشبهة كون فرجه المولج أو المولج فيه أصلياً بخلاف مسائل توريثه مثلاً فإنه لا . الميراث ما لم يتحقق السبب فيعامل بالأضر لعدم تحقق ما يثبت له الأنفع، يدل على ما قلنا ما في كتاب الخنثى من غاية البيان : إذا وقف في صف النساء أحب إلي أن يعيد الصلاة. كذا قال محمد في الأصل لأن المسقط وهو والمفسد وهو المحاذاة الأداء معلوم و موهوم وللتوهم أحب إعادة الصلاة. وإن قام في صف الرجال فصلاته تامة ويعيد من عن يمينه وعن يساره والذي خلفه بحذائه على طريق الاستحباب لتوهم المحاذاة اهـ. ثم عاد إلى مسألة الحيض: عنده لا يجب وإنما يجب عند الانقطاع. ونقل نظيره في المستصفى عن أستاذه وعلل له بأن الخروج منه مستلزم للحيض فقد وجد الاتصال بينهما فصحت الاستعارة. وفي غاية البيان: هذا والله عن عجائب الدنيا لأنه إذا كان الخروج ملزوماً والحيض لازماً يلزم أن يوجد الحيض عند وجود الخروج لاستحالة انفكاك اللازم عن الملزوم ووجد الحيض عند وجوده محال بمرة ا هـ . أقول: ليس في هذا شيء من العجب وما العجب إلا فهم الكلام على وجه يتوجه عليه الاعتراض ولو فهم أن الخروج من الحيض مستلزم لتقدم الحيض لا لنفس الحيض لاستغنى عن هذا الاعتراض واستبعد الزيلعي كون الانقطاع سبباً لأنه ليس فيه إلا الطهارة ومن المحال أن توجب الطهارة الطهارة وإنما توجبها النجاسة، ويدفع هذا الاستبعاد بأن الانقطاع نفسه ليس بطهر إنما الطهر الحالة المستمرة عقيبه ولو سلم، فلما كان الانقطاع لا بد منه في وجوب الغسل إذ لا فائدة في الغسل بدونه نسبت السببية إليه وإن كان السبب في الحقيقة خروج الدم والحاصل أنهم اختلفوا هل الغسل يجب بخروج الدم بشرط الانقطاع أو يجب بنفس الانقطاع؟ ورجح بعضهم الثاني بأن الحيض اسم لدم مخصوص والجوهر لا يكون سبباً للمعنى. والحق غير القولين بل إنما يجب بوجوب كما قدمناه في الوضوء والغسل. وقد نقل الشيخ سراج الدين الهندي الإجماع على أنه لا يجب الوضوء على المحدث، والغسل على الجنب والحائض والنفساء قبل وجوب الصلاة أو إرادة ما لا يحل إلا به، فحينئذ لا فائدة لهذا الخلاف من جهة الإثم فإنهم اتفقوا على عدم الإثم قبل وجوب الصلاة، فظهر بهذا ضعف ما نقله في السراج الوهاج من أنه جعل فائدة الخلاف تظهر فيما إذا انقطع الدم طلوع الشمس وأخرت الغسل إلى وقت الظهر؛ فعند الكرخي وعامة العراقيين تأثم وعند البخاريين لا تأثم. وعلى هذا الخلاف وجوب الوضوء فعند العراقيين يجب الوضوء للحدث وعند البخاريين للصلاة ا.هـ. وقد يقال : إن فائدته تظهر في التعاليق كأن يقول : إن وجب عليك غسل فأنت طالق وقد ظهر لي فائدة أخرى وهي ما إذا استشهدت قبل انقطاع الدم فمن قال السبب نفس الحيض قال إنها تغسل لأن الشهادة لا ترفع ما وجب قبل الموت كالجنابة ومن قال إن السبب انقطاعه قال لا تغسل لعدم وجوب الغسل قبل الموت، وقد صحح في الهداية في باب الشهيد أنها تغسل فكان تصحيحاً لكون السبب الحيض كما لا يخفى وأما دليل وجوب الغسل من الحيض والنفاس فالإجماع نقله صاحب البدائع من أئمتنا والنووي في شرح المهذب عن ابن المنذر وابن جرير الطبري واستدل بعضهم للحيض بقوله تعالى ولا تقربوهن حتى يطهرن﴾ [البقرة: ٢٢٢] ووجه الدلالة أنه يلزمها تمكين الزوج من الوطء ولا يجوز ذلك إلا بالغسل وما لا يتم
His statement: (And menstruation and postpartum) i.e., ghusl is obligatory at menstruation and postpartum. The author differed in his books: is the cause menstruation itself or its cessation? In al-Mustasfa he chose that the cause is seeing blood or its exit, reasoning that when blood occurs, major purity is broken, and ghusl is not required while blood is flowing because it conflicts, so when it stops, ghusl becomes possible and is obligatory due to that previous hadath. As for cessation, it is purity, so it does not necessitate purity. In al-Kafi he chose that the cause is the cessation of blood, not its exit, because... Likewise, they said: if a virgin is sexually intercourse [sic], there is no ghusl on her unless she becomes pregnant due to her ejaculation. The discussion is whether ghusl is obligatory by intercourse with a young girl where there is no barrier except her young age; they differed. The correct view is that if she is such that intercourse would deflower her, it is not obligatory even if the glans is concealed due to lack of desire; otherwise it is obligatory. In short, it restricts the statement of al-Siraj that 'ghusl is obligatory if he does not deflower her' to the condition of removal of her hymen, not absolutely. This is good speech except for his statement 'except if she becomes pregnant' due to what you know from what preceded... His statement: (And if a problematic hermaphrodite inserts his penis...) al-Sharnublali said in Sharh Nur al-Idah al-Kabir: I say it is problematic to treat the hermaphrodite with the harsher [ruling] in his cases; thus ghusl is incumbent on him. I say: treating him with the harsher and precautionary is not always obligatory but may be recommended in some places, including this one. The reason is that his ambiguity gives rise to a doubt, which does not remove what is certainly established because purity was certainly established, so it is not removed by the doubt that his inserted organ or the one inserted into is original, unlike inheritance issues where the cause is not certain and he is treated with the harsher due to lack of certainty of the beneficial. Evidence for what we say is found in the book of the hermaphrodite from Ghayat al-Bayan: If he stands in the women's row, I prefer that he repeat the prayer. Thus said Muhammad in al-Asl because the cancelling factor and the corrupting factor (being in line) are known and doubtful, and due to doubt he repeats the prayer. And if he stands in the men's row, his prayer is complete, but he should repeat based on recommendation due to doubt of alignment... Then returned to the menstruation issue: According to him it is not obligatory but is obligatory at cessation. He transmitted a similar view in al-Mustasfa from his teacher and reasoned that the exit from [menstruation] necessitates menstruation, so there is connection between them, making the metaphor valid. In Ghayat al-Bayan: 'This is by Allah among the wonders of the world because if the exit is a necessary condition and menstruation is a necessary consequence, then menstruation must occur at the time of exit, as the necessary consequence cannot be separated from the condition, but the occurrence of menstruation at its time is impossible.' I say: There is nothing wonderful in this; the wonder is only understanding the speech in a way that invites objection. If he understood that the exit from menstruation necessitates the precedence of menstruation, not menstruation itself, this objection would be unnecessary. al-Zayla'i considered cessation as a cause unlikely because it contains only purity, and it is impossible for purity to necessitate purity; rather purity is necessitated by impurity. This unlikelihood is refuted by saying that cessation itself is not purity; rather purity is the continuous state after it. Even if conceded, since cessation is necessary for the obligation of ghusl—as there is no benefit in ghusl without it—causality is attributed to it even though the real cause is the exit of blood. The result is they differed: is ghusl obligatory by the exit of blood with the condition of cessation, or by cessation itself? Some preferred the second, because menstruation is a name for a specific blood, and a substance cannot be a cause for a meaning. The truth is different from both: rather it becomes obligatory only with obligation as we mentioned in wudu and ghusl. Shaykh Siraj al-Din al-Hindi transmitted consensus that wudu is not obligatory on the person in hadath, nor ghusl on the junub, menstruating woman, or postpartum woman before the obligation of prayer or intending what is only permissible with it. Therefore this disagreement has no benefit in terms of sin, as they agree there is no sin before the obligation of prayer. Thus the weakness is shown of what was transmitted in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj that he made the benefit appear when the blood stops at sunrise and she delays ghusl to Dhuhr: according to al-Karkhi and most Iraqis she sins, according to the Bukharis she does not sin. On this dispute is the obligation of wudu: according to the Iraqis wudu is obligatory for the hadath, according to the Bukharis for the prayer. It may be said: its benefit appears in conditional statements, e.g., 'If ghusl is obligatory on you, you are divorced.' And another benefit occurred to me: if a woman becomes a shahid before the cessation of blood, then whoever says the cause is menstruation itself says she is washed because shahada does not lift what was obligatory before death, like janaba; whoever says the cause is its cessation says she is not washed because no ghusl was obligatory before death. In al-Hidaya in the chapter of the shahid it was corrected that she is washed, which is a correction that the cause is menstruation, as is not hidden. As for the proof of the obligation of ghusl from menstruation and postpartum, it is ijma', transmitted by the author of al-Bada'i from our imams, and al-Nawawi in Sharh al-Muhadhdhab from Ibn al-Mundhir and Ibn Jarir al-Tabari. Some adduced for menstruation the verse: 'And do not approach them until they are pure' (2:222). The aspect of indication is that she must allow her husband intercourse, and that is only permissible with ghusl, and what is not...
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the cause of ghusl from menstruation and postpartum bleeding. The discussion is a critical refinement of the matn. The author (Ibn Nujaym) is noted to have had two different opinions: in al-Mustasfa he held that the cause is the actual exit of blood (ru'yat al-dam), reasoning that blood nullifies major purity (naqd al-tahara al-kubra) but ghusl cannot be performed while blood is flowing, so the obligation arises when the blood stops. In al-Kafi, he instead held that the cause is the cessation itself (inqita'). The passage then critiques both views through various Hanafi scholars. al-Zayla'i found cessation as a cause unlikely because it is itself a form of purity (tahara), and purity cannot obligate purity. However, the author refutes this by distinguishing between the moment of cessation and the subsequent state of purity. The truth (al-haqq) asserted is that ghusl becomes obligatory only when an act requiring purity becomes necessary (e.g., prayer time), similar to the ruling for wudu. The passage concludes by noting that the practical benefit of this dispute is minimal regarding sin (ithm) but appears in cases of conditional statements (ta'liq) and the washing of a female martyr (shahida). It cites al-Hidaya's correction that she is washed, supporting the view that the cause is the blood itself. The proof for ghusl from hayd is ijma' (consensus) and the Quranic verse (2:222).

Key Terms
الموجب (al-mūjib): The cause that makes ghusl obligatory; specifically, whether it is the blood of menstruation or its cessation.
الانقطاع (al-inqitā‘): The cessation of menstrual blood flow.
الاستشهاد (al-istishhād): Death as a martyr (shahīd) in battle.
الشهادة (al-shahāda): Martyrdom for the sake of Allah.
الإثم (al-ithm): Sin; whether a woman sins for delaying ghusl after the cessation of menstruation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the practical benefit of the dispute over the cause of ghusl from menstruation?
A) Whether a woman must perform wudu after her period
B) Whether a female martyr is washed if she dies while menstruating
C) Whether a woman can fast during menstruation
D) Whether a man can marry a menstruating woman

Key Takeaway
The cause of ghusl from menstruation is the blood itself (with cessation as a condition), as supported by the ruling on washing a female martyr in al-Hidayah; the dispute does not affect the core obligation but impacts secondary rulings like sin and conditional divorces.

Taharah — Purification
advanced
12 min
The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on the Cause of Obligatory Ghusl from Menstruation
Intra-Hanafi juridical analysis of the sabab for obligatory ghusl after menstruation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on what triggers obligatory ghusl after menstruation, explain the evidence for each, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) in the case of sin and washing a martyr.

Core Idea
The obligation of ghusl after menstruation is tied to hadath, not cessation; the dispute is over whether the cause is the blood itself or its discontinuation.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq presents a detailed intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the precise moment that ghusl becomes obligatory after menstruation. The discussion hinges on whether the cause (sabab) is the menstrual blood itself (conditioned on cessation) or the cessation of blood flow. This seemingly subtle difference has significant practical implications for when a woman incurs sin for delaying ghusl, and whether a martyr who dies while menstruating should be washed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
The Iraqi school (Al-Karkhi et al.) holds that ghusl is for hadath and becomes obligatory upon cessation, so delaying after cessation incurs sin. The Bukhari school holds it is for prayer, so no sin until prayer time.
2
Locate the evidence for each view
The Iraqis argue that cessation is a condition; the real cause is the blood itself. The Bukharis argue that a substance cannot be a cause; cessation is the actual cause.
3
Analyze the commentary's critique
The author refutes the Bukhari position by showing that cessation itself is not purification; it is a condition. The hadath persists.
4
Examine the thamarah al-khilaf
Two scenarios: (a) delaying ghusl after cessation but before Zawal: Iraqis say she sins, Bukharis say no sin. (b) martyrdom before cessation: if cause is blood, she is washed; if cause is cessation, she is not.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Al-Hidayah supports washing the martyr, which validates the Iraqi position that the cause is the blood itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة ۱۱۳ الصلاة عنده لا يجب وإنما يجب عند الانقطاع. ونقل نظيره في المستصفى عن أستاذه وعلل له بأن الخروج منه مستلزم للحيض فقد وجد الاتصال بينهما فصحت الاستعارة. وفي غاية البيان: هذا والله عن عجائب الدنيا لأنه إذا كان الخروج ملزوماً والحيض لازماً يلزم أن يوجد الحيض عند وجود الخروج لاستحالة انفكاك اللازم عن الملزوم ووجد الحيض عند وجوده محال بمرة ا هـ . أقول: ليس في هذا شيء من العجب وما العجب إلا فهم الكلام على وجه يتوجه عليه الاعتراض ولو فهم أن الخروج من الحيض مستلزم لتقدم الحيض لا لنفس الحيض لاستغنى عن هذا الاعتراض واستبعد الزيلعي كون الانقطاع سبباً لأنه ليس فيه إلا الطهارة ومن المحال أن توجب الطهارة الطهارة وإنما توجبها النجاسة، ويدفع هذا الاستبعاد بأن الانقطاع نفسه ليس بطهر إنما الطهر الحالة المستمرة عقيبه ولو سلم، فلما كان الانقطاع لا بد منه في وجوب الغسل إذ لا فائدة في الغسل بدونه نسبت السببية إليه وإن كان السبب في الحقيقة خروج الدم والحاصل أنهم اختلفوا هل الغسل يجب بخروج الدم بشرط الانقطاع أو يجب بنفس الانقطاع؟ ورجح بعضهم الثاني بأن الحيض اسم لدم مخصوص والجوهر لا يكون سبباً للمعنى. والحق غير القولين بل إنما يجب بوجوب كما قدمناه في الوضوء والغسل. وقد نقل الشيخ سراج الدين الهندي الإجماع على أنه لا يجب الوضوء على المحدث، والغسل على الجنب والحائض والنفساء قبل وجوب الصلاة أو إرادة ما لا يحل إلا به، فحينئذ لا فائدة لهذا الخلاف من جهة الإثم فإنهم اتفقوا على عدم الإثم قبل وجوب الصلاة، فظهر بهذا ضعف ما نقله في السراج الوهاج من أنه جعل فائدة الخلاف تظهر فيما إذا انقطع الدم طلوع الشمس وأخرت الغسل إلى وقت الظهر؛ فعند الكرخي وعامة العراقيين تأثم وعند البخاريين لا تأثم. وعلى هذا الخلاف وجوب الوضوء فعند العراقيين يجب الوضوء للحدث وعند البخاريين للصلاة ا.هـ. وقد يقال : إن فائدته تظهر في التعاليق كأن يقول : إن وجب عليك غسل فأنت طالق وقد ظهر لي فائدة أخرى وهي ما إذا استشهدت قبل انقطاع الدم فمن قال السبب نفس الحيض قال إنها تغسل لأن الشهادة لا ترفع ما وجب قبل الموت كالجنابة ومن قال إن السبب انقطاعه قال لا تغسل لعدم وجوب الغسل قبل الموت، وقد صحح في الهداية في باب الشهيد أنها تغسل فكان تصحيحاً لكون السبب الحيض كما لا يخفى وأما دليل وجوب الغسل من الحيض والنفاس فالإجماع نقله صاحب البدائع من أئمتنا والنووي في شرح المهذب عن ابن المنذر وابن جرير الطبري واستدل بعضهم للحيض بقوله تعالى ولا تقربوهن حتى يطهرن﴾ [البقرة: ٢٢٢] ووجه الدلالة أنه يلزمها تمكين الزوج من الوطء ولا يجوز ذلك إلا بالغسل وما لا يتم الواجب إلا به فهو واجب. وإذا ثبت هذا فيما دون العشرة ثبت في العشرة بدلالة النص وجوب الاغتسال لأجل خروج الدم وقد وجد في العشرة. فإن قيل: إنما وجب الاغتسال فيما دون العشرة لتتأكد به صفة الطهارة عن الحيض وزوال الأذى ليثبت الحل للزوج، ولهذا يثبت الحل بمضي وقت صلاة عليها وإن لم تغتسل لوجود التأكيد بصيرورة الصلاة ديناً عليها وفي العشرة قد تأكد صفة الطهارة بنفس الانقطاع فانعدم المعنى الموجب فلا يمكن الالحاق بطريق الدلالة كما لا يمكن إثبات الحد باللواطة بمعنى الحرمة لانعدام المعنى الموجب للحد بعد الحرمة وهو كثرة الوقوع قلنا ليس كذلك بل المعنى الموجب موجود لأنه إما الحدث أو إرادة الصلاة على الخلاف، وكلاهما ثابت هنا، فأما الفرق الذي يدعيه فإنما يثبت إذا كان وجوب الاغتسال لثبوت الحل وليس كذلك، ألا ترى أنها لو لم تكن ذات زوج وجب عليها الاغتسال مع انعدام المعنى الذي يدعيه ولكنه وإن وجب آخر جعل غاية للحرمة فيما دون العشرة فإن الحيض به ينتهي فتنتهي الحرمة المبنية عليه، فعرفنا بعبارة النص في قراءة التشديد حرمان القربان مغياً إلى الاغتسال فيما دون العشرة بإشارته وجوب الاغتسال وبدلالته وجوبه في العشرة. كذا في معراج الدراية معزياً إلى شيخه العلامة. ويدل عليه أيضاً حديث فاطمة بنت أبي حبيش أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال لها : إذا أقبلت الحيضة فدعي الصلاة وإذا أدبرت فاغتسلي وصلي . رواه البخاري ومسلم عن عائشة. وفي بعض الروايات فاغسلي عنك الدم وصلي وفي البدائع: ولا نص في النفاس وإنما عرف بالإجماع، ثم إجماعهم يجوز أن يكون على خبر في الباب لكنهم تركوا نقله اكتفاء بالإجماع، ويجوز أن يكون بالقياس على دم الحيض لكون كل منهما دماً خارجاً من الرحم ا هـ . والمذكور في الأصول أن الإجماع في كل حادثة لا يتوقف على نص على الأصح. وفي الكافي للحاكم الشهيد وإذا اجنبت المرأة ثم أدركها الحيض فإن شاءت اغتسلت وإن شاءت أخرت حتى تطهر وعند مالك : عليها أن تغتسل بناء على أصله أن الحائض لها أن تقرأ القرآن ففي اغتسالها من الجنابة هذه الفائدة. قوله : (وقد ظهر لي فائدة أخرى الخ) قال في النهر : ولا بد أن يقيه بما إذا استمر بها ثلاثة البحر الرائق/ ج ١/ م ٨ أيام، أما إذا قتلت قبل إتمامها لا تغسل إجماعاً إلا أن هذا قد يعكر على ما سبق عن الهندي فيحمل الاتفاق على وجوب الأداء اهـ .
The Book of Purification. He (Al-Quduri) said: It (ghusl) is not obligatory at the time of the blood, rather it becomes obligatory at the time of cessation. And a similar statement was narrated in Al-Mustasfa from his teacher, and he gave the reason that the exit (of blood) necessitates menstruation, so connection is found between them, and thus the metaphor is correct. In Ghayat al-Bayan: This, by Allah, is one of the wonders of the world, because if exit is a necessary condition and menstruation is a necessary result, then menstruation must occur when the exit occurs, due to the impossibility of the consequent being separate from the condition, and menstruation occurring at its occurrence is absolutely impossible. End quote. I say: There is nothing wondrous in this; the wonder is only in understanding the statement in a way that invites objection. If he understood that the exit from menstruation necessitates the prior existence of menstruation, not menstruation itself, he would not need this objection. Al-Zayla'i considered it unlikely that cessation is the cause, because it contains only purification, and it is impossible for purification to necessitate purification; rather it is impurity that necessitates purification. This unlikeliness is repelled by the fact that cessation itself is not purification; rather purification is the continuous state after it. Even if conceded, since cessation is necessary for the obligation of ghusl (since there is no benefit in ghusl without it), causality is attributed to it, even though the real cause is the exit of blood. The conclusion is that they differed: Is ghusl obligatory by the exit of blood conditioned upon cessation, or by cessation itself? Some preferred the second, because menstruation is the name for specific blood, and a substance cannot be a cause for a meaning. The truth is neither of the two views; rather it becomes obligatory when it becomes obligatory, as we presented in wudu and ghusl. Shaykh Siraj al-Din al-Hindi reported consensus that wudu is not obligatory upon the one in a state of hadath, nor ghusl upon the junub, menstruating, or postpartum woman before the obligation of prayer or intending what is only permitted by it. So there is no benefit in this disagreement regarding sin, for they all agreed on no sin before the obligation of prayer. Thus the weakness is clear of what was reported in Al-Siraj al-Wahhaj that the benefit of the disagreement appears in the case where blood ceases at sunrise and she delays ghusl until Zuhr time: according to al-Karkhi and most Iraqis, she sins; according to the Bukharis, she does not sin. And upon this disagreement is the obligation of wudu: according to the Iraqis, wudu is obligatory for hadath; according to the Bukharis, for prayer. End quote. It may be said: Its benefit appears in conditional statements, like if he says: If ghusl becomes obligatory upon you, you are divorced. And another benefit became apparent to me: that is the case if she is martyred before the cessation of blood. For one who says the cause is menstruation itself says she should be washed, because martyrdom does not remove what was obligatory before death, like janabah. And one who says the cause is its cessation says she is not washed, because ghusl was not obligatory before death. It was corrected in Al-Hidayah in the chapter of the martyr that she is washed, so it is a correction that the cause is menstruation, as is not hidden. As for the evidence for the obligation of ghusl from menstruation and postpartum bleeding, it is consensus, which the author of Al-Bada'i narrated from our imams, and al-Nawawi in Sharh al-Muhadhdhab from Ibn al-Mundhir and Ibn Jarir al-Tabari. And some argued for menstruation by His statement: 'And do not approach them until they are purified' (Al-Baqarah 222). The indication is that it becomes obligatory for her to allow her husband intercourse, and that is not permissible except with ghusl, and what the obligatory act cannot be completed without is itself obligatory. And if this is established for less than ten days, it is established for ten days by implication of the text: the obligation of ghusl due to the exit of blood, and it has occurred in the ten days. If it is said: Ghusl was made obligatory for less than ten days to emphasize the quality of purification from menstruation and removal of harm to establish permissibility for the husband; for this reason, permissibility is established by the passing of a prayer time upon her even if she does not perform ghusl, due to the emphasis by prayer becoming a debt upon her. In the ten days, the quality of purification is emphasized by cessation itself, so the meaning that necessitates it is absent, so analogy cannot be made by implication, just as the hadd punishment cannot be established for sodomy by the meaning of prohibition, because the meaning necessitating the hadd is absent after prohibition, which is frequency of occurrence. We say: It is not so; rather the necessitating meaning exists, because it is either hadath or the intention of prayer, according to the disagreement, and both are present here. As for the distinction he claims, it would be established only if the obligation of ghusl was for establishing permissibility, but it is not so. Do you not see that if she had no husband, ghusl would still be obligatory upon her, even though the meaning he claims is absent? But even if it is obligatory for another reason, it was made a limit for prohibition in less than ten days, so menstruation ends with it, and the prohibition based on it ends. Thus we know from the expression of the text in the reading of tashdid (the prohibition of approach) that it is conditioned upon ghusl for less than ten days, indicating by its indication the obligation of ghusl, and by its implication the obligation for ten days. Thus in Miraj al-Dirayah, attributed to his teacher the scholar. And it is also indicated by the hadith of Fatimah bint Abi Hubaysh that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said to her: 'When the menstruation comes, leave prayer, and when it recedes, perform ghusl and pray.' Reported by Bukhari and Muslim from Aisha. And in some narrations: 'Wash the blood from yourself and pray.' And in Al-Bada'i: There is no text for postpartum bleeding; it is known only by consensus. Then their consensus may be based on a report on the topic but they left its transmission, deeming consensus sufficient, or it may be by analogy with menstrual blood, since both are blood exiting the womb. End quote. And it is mentioned in usul that consensus on every incident does not depend on a text, according to the correct view. And in Al-Kafi by al-Hakim al-Shahid: If a woman becomes junub and then menstruation overtakes her, she may wash if she wishes or delay until she becomes pure. According to Malik, she must wash, based on his principle that a menstruating woman may recite Quran, so there is this benefit in her washing from janabah. His statement: (And another benefit became apparent to me, etc.) He said in Al-Nahr: It must be preserved if she continued for three days. Al-Bahr al-Raiq, vol. 1, p. 8. Days: if she is killed before completing them, she is not washed by consensus, except that this may affect what was stated earlier by al-Hindi, so the agreement is interpreted as on the obligation of performance. End quote.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the cause that triggers the obligation of ghusl after menstruation. The core issue is whether ghusl becomes obligatory by the mere exit of menstrual blood (conditioned upon its cessation) or by the cessation itself. The text begins by reporting that according to Al-Quduri's matn, ghusl is not obligatory at the time of the blood but when the blood stops. The commentator (Ibn Nujaym) then presents various views: some prefer cessation as the cause, but the author sides with the opinion that the obligation arises only when a demand for prayer or similar arises (i.e., it is not a continuous obligation). The dispute between the Iraqi scholars (following Al-Karkhi) and the Bukhari scholars is highlighted: the former say ghusl is for hadath, so sin is incurred if one delays after cessation; the latter say it is for prayer, so no sin until the prayer time. The commentator adds a benefit from this disagreement: if a menstruating woman is martyred before cessation, according to those who say the cause is the blood itself (since it was already present), she must be washed like a junub; according to those who say cessation is the cause, she is not washed. The Hidayah supports washing, validating the view that the cause is the blood. The evidence for ghusl from menstruation is consensus, and a Quranic verse (2:222) is used as an indication: since purification is required for marital relations, and purification through ghusl is necessary, ghusl becomes obligatory. The discussion also covers the extension of the ruling to the full ten days (maximum menstruation) via implication from the text. A counter-argument is refuted: the obligation is not solely for establishing marital permissibility, as evidenced by the case of a woman without a husband. Finally, the passage addresses the case of a woman who becomes junub then gets her period; per the Hanafi view she may delay ghusl, while Malik obligates it due to his view that a menstruating woman may recite Quran.

Key Terms
انقطاع (inqiṭāʿ): The cessation of menstrual blood flow; some consider it the cause for obligatory ghusl.
حيض (ḥayḍ): Menstrual blood that exits the womb during the monthly cycle.
غسل (ghusl): Full-body ritual washing required to remove major impurity (janabah, menstruation, postpartum).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (mufta bihi), a woman who is martyred while menstruating before her blood stops:
A) Is washed because a hadath existed.
B) Is not washed because ghusl was not obligatory.
C) Is washed only if she had intended to pray.
D) The school has no ruling.

Key Takeaway
The obligation of ghusl after menstruation is a hadath-based obligation; the dispute over its cause (blood vs. cessation) has practical consequences for sin and the washing of martyrs.

Things That Do Not Obligate Ghusl: Madhi, Wady, and Ihtilam Without Wetness
Listing substances and conditions that do not require ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which bodily fluids and states require only wudu and which require ghusl in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Only maniy requires ghusl; madhi, wady, and ihtilam without wetness require only wudu.

This lesson clarifies the Hanafi ruling on substances that do not obligate full-body washing (ghusl). The matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq lists three cases: madhi (pre-ejaculate), wady (prostatic fluid), and ihtilam without wetness (nocturnal dream without emission). Understanding these distinctions helps avoid unnecessary ghusl while ensuring proper purification according to the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three items
Locate the Arabic terms: madhi, wady, and ihtilam without wetness. Recognize that they are grammatically conjoined to 'maniy' from a previous line, indicating they share the same ruling of not obligating ghusl.
Note the genitive case in 'la madhi wady wa ihtilam...' shows connection.
2
Understand the ruling
For each of these, ghusl is not wajib. Only wudu is required if one intends to pray or touch the Quran. The ruling is based on the clear text of the matn.
3
Distinguish from maniy
Maniy (semen) is the only fluid that makes ghusl wajib. The distinction is based on physical characteristics and the associated sexual climax.
The commentary defines madhi as a thin white fluid that exits during arousal.
4
Apply to a scenario
If someone experiences madhi during foreplay, they only need to perform wudu and wash the affected area. If they experience a wet dream without any emission, no ghusl is needed.
This prevents unnecessary burden.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وودي واحتلام بلا بلل وسن للجمعة والعيدين والإحرام وعرفة ووجب للميت ولمن لأن بسبب قوله : (لا مذي وودي واحتلام بلا بلل بالجر عطف على مني» أي لا يفترض الغسل عند هذه الأشياء. أما المذي ففيه ثلاث لغات المذي بإسكان الذال وتخفيف الياء والمذي بكسر الذال وتشديد الياء وهاتان مشهورتان. قال الأزهري وغيره : التخفيف أفصح وأكثر . والثالثة المذني بكسر الذال وإسكان الياء حكاها أبو عمر الزاهد في شرح الفصيح عن ابن الإعرابي. ويقال مَذَي بالتخفيف وأمذي ومذي بالتشديد والأوّل أفصح، وهو ماء أبيض
And wady, and ihtilam without wetness, and sunnah for Friday, the two Eids, ihram, and Arafa, and obligatory for the deceased and for one because... His statement: (Not madhi, wady, and ihtilam without wetness – in genitive case as a conjunction to 'maniy') meaning that ghusl is not made obligatory by these things. As for madhi, it has three dialects: al-madhi with sukun of the dhal and tashdid of the ya, and al-madhi with kasra of the dhal and tashdid of the ya; these two are well-known. Al-Azhari and others said: the lightened pronunciation is more eloquent and common. The third is al-madhnī with kasra of the dhal and sukun of the ya; it was narrated by Abu Umar al-Zahid in Sharh al-Fasih from Ibn al-A'rabi. It is also said madha with tashdid and amdhi and madhi with tashdid; the first is most eloquent. It is white water.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, listing substances and states that do not necessitate ghusl (full-body purification). The base text (matn) mentions: wady (prostatic fluid), ihtilam (nocturnal emission) without wetness, and sunnah ghusls for Friday, two Eids, ihram, and Arafa, and obligatory ghusl for the deceased. The commentary focuses on the first part: the words 'la madhi wady wa ihtilam bia balal' are in the genitive case, indicating they are joined to 'maniy' from a previous line (not included in this excerpt), meaning ghusl is not obligatory for these. The commentator then provides extensive linguistic notes on the word 'madhi', explaining its three pronunciations and noting that the lightened form (madhī) is the most eloquent. He defines madhi as a thin white fluid that exits during arousal.

Key Terms
مذي (madhiy): A thin white fluid that exits from the urethra during arousal or foreplay, but not due to orgasm.
ودي (wady): A thick white fluid that may exit after urinating or in some cases of illness.
احتلام بلا بلل (ihtilām bi lā balal): Experiencing a wet dream without the emission of semen.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following requires only wudu, not ghusl?
A) Madhi (pre-ejaculate)
B) Maniy (semen) from sexual intercourse
C) Semen from a wet dream with emission
D) Blood from a wound

Key Takeaway
Madhi, wady, and ihtilam without wetness do not obligate ghusl; only wudu is required.

Woman after Menses: Ghusl Obligation and Delay for Junub
Inter-madhhab dispute on timing of ghusl for a junub menstruating woman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ghusl for a woman after menses and explain why she may delay ghusl if she is junub, contrasting with the Maliki position.

Core Idea
A woman may delay ghusl from janabah until her menses ends; one ghusl suffices for both.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses the obligation of ghusl after menses and the specific case of a woman who is already junub when her period begins. The Hanafi school permits her to wait until her menses ends, while Imam Malik insists on immediate ghusl. Understanding this difference clarifies the interplay between janabah and menstruation in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling: a woman who becomes junub and then menstruates may delay ghusl until she becomes pure (after menses).
This is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Malik holds that she must perform ghusl immediately upon becoming junub, even during menses, because a menstruating woman can still recite Quran.
Malik's principle allows limited acts of worship during menses.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Malik argues that since a menstruating woman may recite Quran, the state of janabah's restriction is not absolute; therefore ghusl from janabah still benefits her and cannot be delayed.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds that the primary purpose of ghusl from janabah is to permit acts that require purity (prayer, fasting, touching Quran), which are prohibited during menses. Hence, the benefit is postponed, and delay is permissible.
Hanafi usul: acts of worship require purity; menses prevents that purity from being effective.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
In practice: a Hanafi woman delays ghusl until her period ends; a Maliki woman must perform ghusl immediately upon janabah even during menses.
This practical difference affects daily life and fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وودي واحتلام بلا بلل وسن للجمعة والعيدين والإحرام وعرفة ووجب للميت ولمن لأن بسبب الواجب إلا به فهو واجب. وإذا ثبت هذا فيما دون العشرة ثبت في العشرة بدلالة النص وجوب الاغتسال لأجل خروج الدم وقد وجد في العشرة. فإن قيل: إنما وجب الاغتسال فيما دون العشرة لتتأكد به صفة الطهارة عن الحيض وزوال الأذى ليثبت الحل للزوج، ولهذا يثبت الحل بمضي وقت صلاة عليها وإن لم تغتسل لوجود التأكيد بصيرورة الصلاة ديناً عليها وفي العشرة قد تأكد صفة الطهارة بنفس الانقطاع فانعدم المعنى الموجب فلا يمكن الالحاق بطريق الدلالة كما لا يمكن إثبات الحد باللواطة بمعنى الحرمة لانعدام المعنى الموجب للحد بعد الحرمة وهو كثرة الوقوع قلنا ليس كذلك بل المعنى الموجب موجود لأنه إما الحدث أو إرادة الصلاة على الخلاف، وكلاهما ثابت هنا، فأما الفرق الذي يدعيه فإنما يثبت إذا كان وجوب الاغتسال لثبوت الحل وليس كذلك، ألا ترى أنها لو لم تكن ذات زوج وجب عليها الاغتسال مع انعدام المعنى الذي يدعيه ولكنه وإن وجب آخر جعل غاية للحرمة فيما دون العشرة فإن الحيض به ينتهي فتنتهي الحرمة المبنية عليه، فعرفنا بعبارة النص في قراءة التشديد حرمان القربان مغياً إلى الاغتسال فيما دون العشرة بإشارته وجوب الاغتسال وبدلالته وجوبه في العشرة. كذا في معراج الدراية معزياً إلى شيخه العلامة. ويدل عليه أيضاً حديث فاطمة بنت أبي حبيش أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال لها : إذا أقبلت الحيضة فدعي الصلاة وإذا أدبرت فاغتسلي وصلي . رواه البخاري ومسلم عن عائشة. وفي بعض الروايات فاغسلي عنك الدم وصلي وفي البدائع: ولا نص في النفاس وإنما عرف بالإجماع، ثم إجماعهم يجوز أن يكون على خبر في الباب لكنهم تركوا نقله اكتفاء بالإجماع، ويجوز أن يكون بالقياس على دم الحيض لكون كل منهما دماً خارجاً من الرحم ا هـ . والمذكور في الأصول أن الإجماع في كل حادثة لا يتوقف على نص على الأصح. وفي الكافي للحاكم الشهيد وإذا اجنبت المرأة ثم أدركها الحيض فإن شاءت اغتسلت وإن شاءت أخرت حتى تطهر وعند مالك : عليها أن تغتسل بناء على أصله أن الحائض لها أن تقرأ القرآن ففي اغتسالها من الجنابة هذه الفائدة.
And the opinion of those who say that ghusl is obligatory for a woman after her menses ends is established by the text for periods less than ten days, and by the implication of the text for the full ten days. It is supported by the hadith of Fatima bint Abi Hubaish, and by consensus. In al-Kafi by al-Hakim al-Shahid: If a woman becomes junub and then menstruation overtakes her, she may perform ghusl if she wishes or delay until she becomes pure. According to Malik, she must perform ghusl based on his principle that a menstruating woman may recite the Quran.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the obligation of ghusl for a woman after her menses ends, and specifically the ruling for a woman who is junub and then menstruates. The Hanafi position: Ghusl is wajib after the cessation of menses, whether before ten days or at ten days, based on the hadith of Fatima and qiyas. For a junub woman who menstruates, she may delay ghusl until her menses ends, as her state of janabah is suspended. Malik holds that she must perform ghusl immediately because, in his view, a menstruating woman is allowed to recite the Quran, so the purpose of ghusl from janabah is not nullified. The Hanafi view is that menstruation prevents the validity of wudu and thus the benefit of ghusl from janabah is postponed.

Key Terms
حيض (hayd): Menstrual blood, the discharge that triggers rulings of impurity and prohibition of prayer.
جنابة (janabah): State of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl.
اغتسال (ightisal): Full-body ritual washing (ghusl).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a woman becomes junub and then menstruates, what is the ruling on ghusl?
A) She must perform ghusl immediately for janabah.
B) She may delay ghusl until she becomes pure from menses.
C) She must perform two ghusls – one for janabah and one for menses.
D) She must perform ghusl only at the end of menses, and it suffices for both.

Key Takeaway
A woman who is junub and then menstruates may delay ghusl until her menses ends; the Hanafi view is relied upon despite Maliki objection.

Ghusl Not Required for Madhi, Wadi, and Ihtilam without Discharge
Ruling with proof on fluids not requiring ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on madhi, wadi, and ihtilam, and apply the hadith of Ali to derive the obligation of wudu.

Core Idea
Madhi, wadi, and ihtilam without discharge do not require ghusl, only wudu.

This lesson clarifies a common point of confusion: not every fluid requiring purification demands a full bath (ghusl). The Hanafi school, along with general scholarly consensus, holds that madhi (pre-ejaculate) and wadi (prostatic fluid) only necessitate wudu. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correct daily worship, especially given the subtle difference between these fluids and semen.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three fluids and their definitions
From the passage, extract the definitions of madhi, wadi, and ihtilam (wet dream without discharge). Madhi is thin white fluid from arousal; wadi is thick cloudy fluid after urine; ihtilam without discharge requires no purification.
Madhi is common in women; wadi is often confused with semen but lacks its odor and force.
2
State the ruling for each
For madhi and wadi: ghusl is not wajib, but wudu is wajib. For ihtilam without discharge: no purification at all. This is the zahir al-riwayah position.
The level of wudu obligation is wajib (not fard) because the evidence is a single hadith and qiyas.
3
Locate the dalil for madhi
The hadith of Ali (ra) recorded in Bukhari and Muslim states that madhi only requires wudu. This is the primary proof for the ruling.
The hadith is zanni al-thubut (single narration) and zanni al-dalala (yields obligation by inference).
4
Understand the question about wadi
The passage addresses a potential objection: if urine already requires wudu, why specify wadi? The answer includes several benefits: cases of urinary incontinence, sequential wudu, and the principle that wudu from separate causes counts for both.
This shows the thoroughness of Hanafi legal reasoning in anticipating and resolving doubts.
5
Identify the consensus
The text states that scholars (aimma) are unanimous that ghusl is not required for madhi and wadi. This ijma strengthens the ruling.
Consensus (ijma) makes the ruling even more authoritative, though the primary proof is the hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لا مذي وودي واحتلام بلا بلل بالجر عطف على مني» أي لا يفترض الغسل عند هذه الأشياء. أما المذي ففيه ثلاث لغات المذي بإسكان الذال وتخفيف الياء والمذي بكسر الذال وتشديد الياء وهاتان مشهورتان. قال الأزهري وغيره : التخفيف أفصح وأكثر . والثالثة المذني بكسر الذال وإسكان الياء حكاها أبو عمر الزاهد في شرح الفصيح عن ابن الإعرابي. ويقال مَذَي بالتخفيف وأمذي ومذي بالتشديد والأوّل أفصح، وهو ماء أبيض رقيق يخرج عند شهوة لا بشهوة ولا دفق ولا يعقبه فتور وربما لا يحس بخروجه وهو أغلب في النساء. وفي بعض الشروح أن ما يخرج من المرأة عند الشهوة يسمى القذي بمفتوحتين. والوذي بإسكان الدال المهملة وتخفيف الياء ولا يجوز عند جمهور أهل اللغة غير هذا، وحكى الجوهري في الصحاح عن الأموي أنه قال بتشديد الياء، وحكى صاحب مطالع الأنوار لغة أنه بالذال المعجمة وهذان شاذان. يقال ودي بتخفيف الدال وأودي وودي بالتشديد والأول أفصح ، وهو ماء أبيض كدر ثخين يشبه المني في الثخانة ويخالفه في الكدورة ولا رائحة له، ويخرج عقيب البول إذا كانت الطبيعة مستمسكة وعند حمل شيء ثقيل ويخرج قطرة أو قطرتين ونحوهما، وأجمع العلماء أنه لا يجب الغسل بخروج المذي والودي. كذا في شرح المهذب. وإذا لم يجب بهما الغسل وجب بهما الوضوء. وفي المذي حديث علي المشهور الصحيح الثابت في البخاري ومسلم وغيرهما فإن قيل : ما فائدة إيجاب الوضوء بالودي وقد وجب بالبول السابق عليه ؟ قلنا عن ذلك أجوبة : أحدها فائدته فيمن به سلس البول فإن الودي ينقض وضوءه دون البول. ثانيها فيمن توضأ عقب البول قبل خروج الودي ثم خرج الودي فيجب به الوضوء. ثالثها يجب الوضوء لو تصوّر الانتقاض به كما فرع أبو حنيفة مسائل المزارعة لو كان يقول بجوازها قال في الغاية وفيه ضعف ورابعها الودي ما يخرج بعد الاغتسال من الجماع وبعد البول وهو شيء لزج كذا فسره في الخزانة ،والتبيين فالإشكال إنما يرد على من اقتصر في تفسيره على ما يخرج بعد البول. خامسها أن وجوب الوضوء بالبول لا ينافي الوجوب بالودي بعده ويقع الوضوء عنهما حتى لو حلف لا يتوضأ من رعاف فرعف ثم بال أو عكسه فتوضأ فالوضوء منهما فيحنث، وكذا لو حلفت لا تغتسل من جنابة أو حيض فجامعها زوجها وحاضت فاغتسلت فهو منهما وتحنث وهذا ظاهر الرواية.
His statement: 'Not madhi, wadi, and ihtilam without discharge' means that ghusl is not obligatory for these things. As for madhi, it has three pronunciations... And the scholars agree that ghusl is not obligatory for madhi and wadi. If ghusl is not required, then wudu is required for them. Regarding madhi, there is the well-known authentic hadith of Ali in Bukhari and Muslim. If it is asked: What is the benefit of requiring wudu for wadi when wudu is already required by the preceding urine? We answer... and this is the apparent position (zahir al-riwayah).
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the types of impurities that do not require ghusl. The matn states that ghusl is not required for madhi (pre-ejaculatory fluid), wadi (prostatic fluid), and ihtilam (wet dream) without discharge. The commentary provides definitions and pronunciation of these terms. For madhi and wadi, there is consensus that ghusl is not required, but wudu is required. The hadith of Ali confirms this for madhi. The passage then discusses a potential objection: if wudu is already required by urine, why mention wadi? The answer provides five benefits, including cases like urine incontinence, sequential events, and the authoritative view (zahir al-riwayah) that wudu from separate causes suffices and counts for both.

Key Terms
مذي (madhi): A thin, white fluid that exits during arousal, not with force or pleasure, and is common among women.
ودي (wadi): A thick, cloudy white fluid resembling semen but with no odor, often excreted after urine or after ghusl from intercourse.
احتلام (ihtilam): Wet dream; if no discharge is felt, no purification is required.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Madhi and wadi do not necessitate ghusl; only wudu is required, based on the hadith of Ali and scholarly consensus.
