Multiple Causes of Hadath: Do They Require Separate Wudu?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on multiple nullifiers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on multiple nullifiers of wudu and distinguish between the views of al-Jurjani and al-Hindwani.

Core Idea
Once hadath is established, subsequent nullifiers are redundant—a single wudu suffices.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether multiple causes of ritual impurity require multiple wudu. While some scholars differentiate based on cause type, the relied-upon view holds that the first wudu suffices for all subsequent nullifiers. Understanding this dispute reveals a key principle of Hanafi fiqh: hadath is a single legal state that cannot be duplicated.

Guided Path
1
State the two positions
Al-Jurjani holds that wudu from the first cause suffices absolutely; al-Hindwani distinguishes by cause type: if causes are homogeneous (e.g., two urinations), first wudu suffices; if heterogeneous (e.g., urine then nosebleed), separate wudu are required.
The difference hinges on the nature of the causes, not the number.
2
Identify the proof for al-Hindwani's differentiation
Al-Hindwani argues that different types of nullifiers each create a distinct hadath, so each must be removed by a separate wudu.
This view gives weight to the type of cause rather than the state of impurity.
3
Analyze al-Jurjani's proof
Al-Jurjani argues that once the first cause establishes hadath, a second cause cannot re-establish it because 'achieving what is already obtained is impossible' (tahsil al-hasil muhal). The nullifier's effect is to create hadath; if hadath already exists, additional nullifiers are redundant.
This is a logical argument based on the unity of hadath as a legal state.
4
Understand the exception and tarjih
If multiple causes occur simultaneously, each is considered an independent cause and collectively establish hadath. The muhaqqiq (al-Kamal ibn al-Humam) prefers al-Jurjani's view, making it the mufta bihi.
Simultaneous causes are a special case; the general rule is sequential causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الجرجاني : الطهارة من الأول دون الثاني مطلقاً. وقال الهندواني : إن اتحد الجنس كأن بال ثم بال فالوضوء الأول، وإن اختلف كأن بال ثم رعف فالوضوء منهما ذكره في الذخيرة. وقد رجح المحقق في فتح القدير تبعاً للآمدي قول الجرجاني لأن الناقض يثبت الحدث، ثم تجب إزالته عند وجود شروطه وهو أمر واحد لا تعدّد في أسبابه فالثابت بكل سبب هو الثابت بالآخر إذ لا دليل يوجب ذلك فالناقض الأول لما أثبت الحدث لم يعمل الثاني شيئاً لاستحالة تحصيل الحاصل. نعم لو وقعت الأسباب دفعة أضيف ثبوته إلى كلها ولا ينفي ذلك كون كل علة مستقلة لأن معنى الاستقلال كون الوصف بحيث لو انفرد أثر هذه الحيثية ثابتة لكل في حال الاجتماع، وهذا أمر معقول يجب قبوله والحق أحق أن يتبع ويجب حمله على الحكم بتعدد الحكم هنا ولا يستلزم أن يقال به كل موضع لأنه يرفع وقوع تعدّد العلل بحكم واحد وهم في الأصول يثبتونه . خلاف قوله : (ويجب حمله على الحكم بتعدد الحكم الخ) هذا لا ارتباط له بتوجيه قول الجرجاني إذ هو
Al-Jurjani said: Purification is from the first [cause] only, not from the second, absolutely. Al-Hindwani said: If the causes are of the same type, like urinating then urinating [again], the wudu is for the first; if they differ, like urinating then nosebleed, then wudu is for both. The muhaqqiq in Fath al-Qadir, following al-Amidi, preferred al-Jurjani's view because the nullifier establishes hadath, then its removal is required when conditions exist, and it is a single matter with no multiplicity of causes. So what is established by each cause is the same as what is established by the other, as there is no evidence requiring multiplicity. Thus the first nullifier, having established hadath, the second does nothing because getting what is already obtained is impossible. However, if the causes occur simultaneously, then its establishment is attributed to all, and this does not negate each being an independent cause, because independence means the description would have this effect if alone, and this reasoning is sound.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether multiple causes of ritual impurity (hadath) require multiple wudu or only one. Al-Jurjani holds that the first cause creates the hadath, and subsequent causes are redundant; the first wudu suffices for all unless the causes are simultaneous. Al-Hindwani differentiates: if the causes are homogeneous (e.g., two urinations), the first wudu suffices; if heterogeneous (e.g., urination and nosebleed), separate wudu is required. The preferred view, according to al-Kamal ibn al-Humam, is that of al-Jurjani, based on the principle that once hadath is established, it cannot be re-established. This ruling is specific to hadath and does not apply to all multiple causes. The text is incomplete at the end.

Key Terms
حدث (hadath): State of minor ritual impurity that nullifies wudu.
ناقض (naqid): A cause that nullifies wudu, such as urine, nosebleed, etc.
علة (illah): Cause or effective reason; here, the reason for requiring wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in the Hanafi school, if someone urinates and then has a nosebleed before performing wudu, what is the ruling?
A) He must perform two separate wudu: one for urine and one for nosebleed.
B) A single wudu suffices for both.
C) No wudu is required because the first cause already nullified purity.
D) He must perform ghusl because of the nosebleed.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, sequential nullifiers of wudu do not multiply the hadath; a single wudu removes all.

Ruling on Madhi and Wadhi and Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Multiple Hadath Causes
Explains rulings of madhi and wadhi and intra-Hanafi dispute on multiple causes of hadath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on madhi and wadhi, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on multiple hadath causes, and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Madhi and wadhi require wudu, not ghusl; multiple hadath causes do not multiply the hadath.

This lesson covers the rulings on madhi (pre-ejaculate) and wadhi (post-urine fluid). While ghusl is not required, wudu becomes obligatory. A deeper intra-Hanafi dispute arises when multiple causes of hadath occur sequentially: does one wudu suffice or must multiple be performed? The passage presents both sides and the preferred view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rulings for madhi and wadhi
From the passage, note that there is consensus that ghusl is not required for either fluid, but wudu is wajib. Understand the basis: the hadith of Ali (ra) for madhi, and analogy for wadhi.
The five answers explain why wudu for wadhi is beneficial even if urine already required it.
2
Understand the five answers
The passage gives five scenarios where requiring wudu for wadhi is meaningful: (1) urinary incontinence, (2) wudu performed before wadhi exits, (3) hypothetical nullification, (4) wadhi defined as after ghusl/janabah, (5) multiple causes can be covered by one wudu for oath purposes.
These answers show the practical implications of the ruling.
3
Grasp the intra-Hanafi dispute
Al-Jurjani holds that only the first cause of hadath matters; subsequent causes do not require a new wudu. Al-Hindawani distinguishes: if same type (e.g., urine then urine), one wudu; if different types (e.g., urine then nosebleed), multiple wudu are needed. The passage then presents the tarjih from Fath al-Qadir.
This is a dispute within the Hanafi school itself, not between schools.
4
Analyze the reasoning for the preferred view
The preferred view (Jurjani) argues that hadath is a single legal state. Once established by the first cause, a second cause cannot create a new hadath because it is already present. This is based on the principle that establishing what is already established is impossible (tahsil al-hasil).
The passage also addresses an objection from oath-breaking examples, explaining that those are based on custom, not on multiple hadath.
5
Apply the tarjih
Ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir, following al-Amidi, gives tarjih to Jurjani's view. Thus, the operative (mufta bihi) position is that if multiple causes of hadath occur, one wudu suffices for all.
This is the ruling a Hanafi mufti would give today.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النساء[؟] رقيق يخرج عند شهوة لا بشهوة ولا دفق ولا يعقبه فتور وربما لا يحس بخروجه وهو أغلب في من الرجال. وفي بعض الشروح أن ما يخرج من المرأة عند الشهوة يسمى القذي بمفتوحتين. والوذي بإسكان الدال المهملة وتخفيف الياء ولا يجوز عند جمهور أهل اللغة غير هذا، وحكى الجوهري في الصحاح عن الأموي أنه قال بتشديد الياء، وحكى صاحب مطالع الأنوار لغة أنه بالذال المعجمة وهذان شاذان. يقال ودي بتخفيف الدال وأودي وودي بالتشديد والأول أفصح ، وهو ماء أبيض كدر ثخين يشبه المني في الثخانة ويخالفه في الكدورة ولا رائحة له، ويخرج عقيب البول إذا كانت الطبيعة مستمسكة وعند حمل شيء ثقيل ويخرج قطرة أو قطرتين ونحوهما، وأجمع العلماء أنه لا يجب الغسل بخروج المذي والودي. كذا في شرح المهذب. وإذا لم يجب بهما الغسل وجب بهما الوضوء. وفي المذي حديث علي المشهور الصحيح الثابت في البخاري ومسلم وغيرهما فإن قيل : ما فائدة إيجاب الوضوء بالودي وقد وجب بالبول السابق عليه ؟ قلنا عن ذلك أجوبة : أحدها فائدته فيمن به سلس البول فإن الودي ينقض وضوءه دون البول. ثانيها فيمن توضأ عقب البول قبل خروج الودي ثم خرج الودي فيجب به الوضوء. ثالثها يجب الوضوء لو تصوّر الانتقاض به كما فرع أبو حنيفة مسائل المزارعة لو كان يقول بجوازها قال في الغاية وفيه ضعف ورابعها الودي ما يخرج بعد الاغتسال من الجماع وبعد البول وهو شيء لزج كذا فسره في الخزانة ،والتبيين فالإشكال إنما يرد على من اقتصر في تفسيره على ما يخرج بعد البول. خامسها أن وجوب الوضوء بالبول لا ينافي الوجوب بالودي بعده ويقع الوضوء عنهما حتى لو حلف لا يتوضأ من رعاف فرعف ثم بال أو عكسه فتوضأ فالوضوء منهما فيحنث، وكذا لو حلفت لا تغتسل من جنابة أو حيض فجامعها زوجها وحاضت فاغتسلت فهو منهما وتحنث وهذا ظاهر الرواية. وقال الجرجاني : الطهارة من الأول دون الثاني مطلقاً. وقال الهندواني : إن اتحد الجنس كأن بال ثم بال فالوضوء الأول، وإن اختلف كأن بال ثم رعف فالوضوء منهما ذكره في الذخيرة. وقد رجح المحقق في فتح القدير تبعاً للآمدي قول الجرجاني لأن الناقض يثبت الحدث، ثم تجب إزالته عند وجود شروطه وهو أمر واحد لا تعدّد في أسبابه فالثابت بكل سبب هو الثابت بالآخر إذ لا دليل يوجب ذلك فالناقض الأول لما أثبت الحدث لم يعمل الثاني شيئاً لاستحالة تحصيل الحاصل. نعم لو وقعت الأسباب دفعة أضيف ثبوته إلى كلها ولا ينفي ذلك كون كل علة مستقلة لأن معنى الاستقلال كون الوصف بحيث لو انفرد أثر هذه الحيثية ثابتة لكل في حال الاجتماع، وهذا أمر معقول يجب قبوله والحق أحق أن يتبع ويجب حمله على الحكم بتعدد الحكم هنا ولا يستلزم أن يقال به كل موضع لأنه يرفع وقوع تعدّد العلل بحكم واحد وهم في الأصول يثبتونه . خلاف قوله : (ويجب حمله على الحكم بتعدد الحكم الخ) هذا لا ارتباط له بتوجيه قول الجرجاني إذ هو مخالف له بل راجع إلى القول الأول وحاصله أن كل ناقض موجب لحكمه إلا أنه اكتفى بوضوء واحد. ولا يلزم . منه أن يقال به في كل موضع تعددت فيه العلل لحكم واحد لأنه يلزم عليه رفع وقوعها كذلك مع أن الأصوليين أثبتوه، ولا يخفى أن ما ذكره عن الفتح من أن الحدث واحد لا تعدد في أسبابه ينفي ما ذكره وكان الذي حمله على ذلك ما قدمه من مسألة الحنث فإنها تقتضي تعدد الحكم لكن المحقق في فتح القدير قد أجاب عن . ذلك فقال : والحق أن لا تنافي بين كون الحدث بالسبب الأول فقط وبين الحنث لأنه لا يلزم بناؤه على تعدد الحدث بل على العرف، والعرف أن يقال (۱) رواه البخاري في كتاب الوضوء باب ٤٦ مسلم في كتاب الطهارة حديث ۸، ۱۲. أبو داود في كتاب الطهارة باب ،٣٢ ٥١ الترمذي في كتاب الطهارة باب ٤٥ النسائي في كتاب الجمعة باب ۹. أحمد في مسنده (۹۸/۲).
A thin fluid emerges at desire without desire, without gush, without subsequent lethargy, and sometimes one does not feel its exit, and it is more common in men. In some commentaries, what emerges from a woman at desire is called 'qadhi' (with two fatha). 'Wadhi' is with a silent dal and lightened ya; the majority of linguists allow no other pronunciation. Al-Jawhari in al-Sihah reported from al-Amawi that he said with a shaddah on the ya, and the author of Matali' al-Anwar reported a variant with a dhal; these are irregular. It is said 'wadi' with lightened dal, 'awdi', and 'waddi' with shaddah; the first is most eloquent. It is a turbid, thick, white fluid resembling semen in thickness but differing in turbidity, having no smell. It emerges after urination when the bowels are constipated, or when carrying something heavy, and it emerges as one or two drops or the like. Scholars unanimously agree that ghusl is not required upon the exit of madhi and wadhi, as in Sharh al-Muhadhdhab. Since ghusl is not required, wudu is required. For madhi, there is the famous authentic hadith of Ali (ra) established in Bukhari and Muslim. If it is said: What is the benefit of requiring wudu for wadhi when it is already required due to prior urination? We reply with several answers: First, its benefit is for one with urinary incontinence, for wadhi nullifies his wudu, unlike urine. Second, for one who made wudu after urination before wadhi exited, then wadhi exited, requiring wudu. Third, wudu would be required if nullification is imagined, as Abu Hanifa derived the issues of muzara'ah if he allowed it; said in al-Ghayah: this is weak. Fourth, wadhi is what emerges after ghusl from intercourse and after urination, something viscous, as interpreted in al-Khazanah and al-Tabyin; thus the problem only arises for those who restrict its interpretation to what emerges after urination. Fifth, the obligation of wudu for urine does not negate the obligation for wadhi after it, and wudu covers both; so if one swore not to make wudu from a nosebleed and then had a nosebleed and then urinated, or the reverse, and then made wudu, the wudu is from both, so he breaks his oath. Likewise, if a woman swore not to perform ghusl from janabah or hayd, then her husband had intercourse with her and she had hayd, then she performed ghusl, it is from both and she breaks her oath. This is the apparent narration. Al-Jurjani said: purification is from the first only, not the second, absolutely. Al-Hindawani said: if the type is the same, e.g., urination then urination, wudu is from the first; if different, e.g., urination then nosebleed, wudu is from both; as mentioned in al-Dhakhirah. The muhaqqiq in Fath al-Qadir, following al-Amidi, preferred al-Jurjani's statement, because the nullifier establishes hadath, then its removal becomes obligatory when its conditions are present, and it is a single matter with no multiplicity in its causes. What is established by each cause is the same as established by the other, since no evidence necessitates otherwise. So the first nullifier, once it established hadath, the second does nothing due to impossibility of obtaining what is already obtained. Yes, if the causes occur simultaneously, its establishment is attributed to all of them, and that does not negate each cause being independent, because the meaning of independence is that the description is such that if it were alone, it would have effect; this characteristic is fixed for each in the case of combination. This is a reasonable matter that must be accepted; truth is most deserving of being followed, and it must be interpreted as ruling on multiplicity of the ruling here, and it does not require saying so in every place because it would entail the occurrence of multiple causes with a single ruling, while the usuliyyun affirm that. Dispute over his statement: 'And it must be interpreted as ruling on multiplicity of the ruling etc.' This has no connection to justifying al-Jurjani's statement, for it contradicts it; rather it goes back to the first opinion. Its summary is that each nullifier necessitates its ruling except that one wudu suffices. And it does not necessitate saying so in every place where multiple causes occur for a single ruling, because it would entail their occurrence as such, while the usuliyyun affirm it. It is not hidden that what he mentioned from Fath al-Qadir—that hadath is one, no multiplicity in its causes—contradicts what he mentioned. What compelled him to that was the issue of the oath he presented, for it necessitates multiplicity of the ruling. However, the muhaqqiq in Fath al-Qadir replied: The truth is that there is no contradiction between hadath being from the first cause only and the breaking of the oath, because it is not necessarily based on multiplicity of hadath but on custom; custom is that it is said: narrated by Bukhari in Kitab al-Wudu, Bab 46; Muslim in Kitab al-Taharah, Hadith 8,12; Abu Dawud in Kitab al-Taharah, Bab 32,51; al-Tirmidhi in Kitab al-Taharah, Bab 45; al-Nasa'i in Kitab al-Jumu'ah, Bab 9; Ahmad in his Musnad (2/98).
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the rulings on madhi and wadhi and the effect of multiple causes of hadath. It begins by defining wadhi and mentioning the consensus that ghusl is not required for these fluids, but wudu is wajib. The author then presents a question about the benefit of requiring wudu for wadhi when urine already requires it. Five answers are given, highlighting scenarios such as urinary incontinence or sequential emissions. The main dispute concerns when multiple hadath causes occur: does one wudu suffice or are multiple required? Al-Jurjani holds that only the first cause matters; al-Hindawani distinguishes based on whether the type is the same or different. The muhaqqiq in Fath al-Qadir, following al-Amidi, prefers al-Jurjani's view, arguing that hadath is a single state not multiplied by multiple causes. The passage concludes with a response to a potential conflict with oath-breaking, explaining that the ruling on oaths is based on custom, not on multiple hadath.

Key Terms
المذي (al-madhy): A thin fluid emerging during arousal without gush.
الودي (al-wady): A thick turbid fluid emerging after urination or after ghusl from intercourse.
الحدث (al-hadath): Legal impurity that nullifies wudu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi position (mufta bihi), if a person experiences a nosebleed (ru'af) after already being in a state of hadath from urination (without having performed wudu in between), how many wudu are required?
A) Two wudu: one for the urine and one for the nosebleed.
B) One wudu suffices for both.
C) Wudu is not required until prayer time.
D) Only the nosebleed requires wudu, the urine does not.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, hadath is a single state; multiple causes do not necessitate multiple wudu; the mufta bihi position is that one wudu suffices for all sequential causes.

beginner

Ghusl for Wet Dream: The Condition of Discharge
States ruling on ghusl for wet dream and provides hadith evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ghusl after a wet dream and explain the hadith evidence that conditions ghusl on the presence of discharge.

Core Idea
No ghusl for a wet dream unless discharge is seen — the Prophet conditioned it on 'seeing water'.

This lesson addresses a common question: does a wet dream (ihtilam) always require ghusl? The Hanafi school, relying on a clear hadith from Umm Salama, holds that ghusl is only obligatory if discharge is actually observed. We will examine the key proof, address a weak counter-hadith, and clarify how the Hanafis handle the conditional phrasing without relying on the controversial principle of mafhum al-shart.

Guided Path
1
Define the act and its typical outcome
Identify what ihtilam is and what usually accompanies it (discharge). Distinguish between the dream itself and the physical sign.
The term ihtilam came to be used specifically for dreams involving intercourse, where discharge often occurs.
2
State the ruling from the passage
Extract the ruling: ghusl is not obligatory if no discharge occurs. This is the mufta bihi position.
The ruling is derived from the hadith's conditional phrase 'if she sees water'.
3
Analyze the primary proof (Umm Salama hadith)
Look at the hadith text: the Prophet (s) said 'yes, if she sees water.' Identify the conditional particle 'idha' and how it ties the ruling to the presence of discharge.
The hadith is sahih (Bukhari and Muslim), so it is a strong evidence even though it is ahad.
4
Examine the rebuttal of the weak hadith and the usul objection
Note the weak hadith about a man finding wetness without remembering a dream, which some use to argue for ghusl in all cases. The author declares it weak and says the Umm Salama hadith suffices. Then understand the objection that Hanafis do not accept mafhum al-shart and the response: the ruling is suspended on the condition; when the condition is absent, the ruling is absent by original absence (al-'adam al-asli), not by inference.
This is a key point: the Hanafi school does not rely on the controversial concept of mafhum al-shart here but on the explicit ta'liq (suspension) of the ruling on the condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الاحتلام فهو افتعال من الحلم بضم الحاء وإسكان اللام وهو ما يراه النائم من المنامات. يقال حَلَم في منامه بفتح الحاء واللام واحتلم وحلمت كذا وحلمت بكذا هذا أصله ثم جعل اسماً لما يراه النائم من الجماع فيحدث معه إنزال المني غالباً فغلب لفظ الاحتلام في هذا دون غيره من أنواع المنام لكثرة الاستعمال. وحكمه عدم وجوب الغسل إذا لم ينزل لما روى البخاري ومسلم عن أم سلمة رضي الله عنها قالت: جاءت أم سليم امرأة أبي طلحة إلى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فقالت : يا رسول الله إن الله لا يستحي من الحق هل على المرأة من غسل إذا احتلمت؟ قال : نعم إذا رأت الماء. ونقل النووي في شرح المهذب عن ابن المنذر الإجماع عليه. وأما ما استدل به في بعض الشرح ومن حديث عائشة أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم سأل عن الرجل يجد البلل ولا يذكر الاحتلام قال : يغتسل وعن الرجل يرى أنه احتلم ولا يجد البلل قال : لا غسل عليه . فهو وإن كان مشهوراً رواه الدارمي وأبو داود الترمذي وغيرهم لكنه من بن عمر العمري وهو ضعيف عند أهل العلم لا يحتج بروايته ويغني عنه حديث أم سليم المتقدّم فإنه يدل على جميع ما يدل عليه هذا هكذا في شرح المهذب. ولا يقال إن الاستدلال بحديث أم سليم صحيح على مذهب من يقول بمفهوم الشرط وأنتم لا تقولون به لأنا نقول : إن الحكم معلق بالشرط فإذا عدم الشرط انعدم الحكم بالعدم الأصلي لا بأن عدم الشرط أثر في عدم الحكم كما تقدّم .
As for ihtilam (wet dream), it is a verbal noun from h-l-m with damm on ha and sukun on lam; it is what the sleeper sees in dreams. It is said halama fi manamihi with fatha on ha and lam, ihtalama, and halimtu kadha, halimtu bikadha; this is its origin. Then it became a name for what the sleeper sees of intercourse, and usually ejaculation occurs with it. So the term ihtilam became prevalent for this type of dream due to frequent usage. Its ruling is that ghusl is not obligatory if no discharge occurs, because Bukhari and Muslim narrated from Umm Salama (ra) who said: Umm Sulaym, the wife of Abu Talha, came to the Prophet (s) and said: 'O Messenger of Allah, Allah is not ashamed of the truth: is there ghusl for a woman if she has a wet dream?' He said: 'Yes, if she sees water.' Al-Nawawi in Sharh al-Muhadhdhab reported from Ibn al-Mundhir the consensus on this. As for what some commentaries adduced from the hadith of Aisha that the Prophet (s) asked about a man who finds wetness but does not recall a wet dream—he said: he should perform ghusl—and about a man who sees that he had a wet dream but does not find wetness—he said: no ghusl upon him—although it is famous, narrated by al-Darimi, Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, etc., it is from ibn 'Umar al-Amri and is weak according to scholars; his narration is not acceptable as evidence. The hadith of Umm Salama suffices, for it indicates all that this one indicates. This is from Sharh al-Muhadhdhab. And it cannot be said that the inference from the hadith of Umm Salama is only valid for those who accept the concept of the condition (mafhum al-shart) while you (Hanafis) do not accept it, because we say: the ruling is suspended on a condition; when the condition is absent, the ruling is absent by original absence, not because the absence of the condition has an effect on the absence of the ruling, as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling with proof regarding ghusl for a wet dream (ihtilam). The author defines ihtilam and states the ruling: ghusl is not obligatory unless there is discharge ('seeing water'). The primary evidence is the hadith of Umm Salama (ra) where the Prophet (s) said 'yes, if she sees water', implying that without seeing water, no ghusl. The passage also addresses a weak hadith that seems to contradict, explaining it is weak and that the strong hadith suffices. Additionally, the author refutes the objection that the Hanafi school does not accept the concept of condition (mafhum al-shart), explaining that the absence of the condition leads to the absence of the ruling by original absence, not by implication.

Key Terms
الاحتلام (al-ihtilam): A dream involving intercourse, usually resulting in ejaculation.
مفهوم الشرط (mafhum al-shart): The implicative meaning of a condition, i.e., if the condition is absent, the ruling is absent.
التعليق (al-ta'liq): Suspension of a ruling on a condition; the ruling exists only when the condition is fulfilled.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is ghusl required after a wet dream?
A) Every time one remembers a wet dream.
B) Only if there is discharge (seeing water).
C) Only for men, not for women.
D) Always required, as wet dreams are a form of janabah.

Key Takeaway
No ghusl is required after a wet dream unless discharge is seen, based on the Prophet's explicit conditional statement.
